### Post by ofb on 2007-11-07
This is a light-hearted thread for the OS you hated. 

It's for people who've used at *least* three OS on a daily basis over the years. It's not a technical discussion of Pros & Cons, it's just for what you remember the least fondly.

----

I started in the seventies, and while Win 3.1 was definitely a low point I think I have to make a surprise choice of Apple OS 8.5. 

Apologies to all the Mac fans! I tried to like it, I really tried to understand the attraction, but it was just ghastly for me on a PowerMac 7600/120. It's definitely the OS I never, ever want to boot again.

---

### Post by Sisophon2001 on 2007-11-08
I date back to Apple Macintoshes in the mid 1980's, and the worst OS I used was a very early version WinXP pro, and the the best version of windows was the latest release of WinXP Home. That siad, on average I consider WinXP to be a very solid contender. I rember the later years better than the first.

I used Win98 at work long after I had WinXP at home, so my memories of Win98 are not so fond. It was difficult to work with the constant crashes.

I never understood why floks complained about WinME. For me it was an update to Win98, nothing more. My home computers tend to be more stable than work computers, so that could have contributed to the crashes.

So looking back, Win 3.1 was the worst OS I used. I was draged in screeming from the dark world of DOS where I was perfectly happy with my command line and individual colection of apps. Having worked with Apple Macintoshes it was clearly an inferiour OS, and unstable compared with DOS which had the virtue of being cheap and reliable.

Notice no mention of Linux. I have used Linux for two years or more at home, and it is unlikely that I will be changing to someting else soon, so obviously I am happy with it.

Garvan

### Post by HotShotDJ on 2007-11-08
Hmmm... thats a hard one.  I'll not include whatever it was that the University of Connecticut was using back in 1982 -- all I remember about that was that in my Intro to Computer Science class, we used punch cards and had to stand in line at the card reader, and then again at the line-printer just to find out that there was a typo on one of the cards (which I'd have to find, then go down the hall to the punch card machines, fix the error, rinse & repeat).  Before that, in high school, we used TTY terminals, and a roll of paper ticker-tape for saving programs (the machine punched holes in the ticker-tape, and another machine would read the tape to load your program).  Again, the technology, not the OS, was the problem there.  (I laugh at the "Linux is too hard" crowd when I think about those days!)

CP/M, MS-DOS & IBM-DOS never gave me problems.  I liked OS/2 Version 3.  Then I got my first real Windows computer (a 400 MHz Celeron from CompUSA) which came with Windows 98 First Edition -- Not too bad, but it wasn't OS/2 either!  "Upgraded" to Windows ME, and surprisingly I didn't have as many problems as other people did -- but then again, I didn't have high expectations of Microsoft after my OS/2 --> Win98 experience.  Windows 2000 was a breath of fresh air -- FINALLY, a mainstream Windows OS that was as good as my old OS/2 machine.  Then XP came along.  ENOUGH!!!  Switched to Red Hat Linux (I forget what version -- 6.something).  I have since used SuSE, Gentoo & Kubuntu. (I still use Windows 2000 via VirtualBox -- the same full retail license I had seven years ago -- I almost wish I still had my old OS/2 installation disks to play with in VirtualBox!)

By far, the worse OS I ever attempted to use was Red Hat Linux 7.3 -- whatever they did from version 7.2 --> 7.3 completely borked my system.  I never was able to get that version to function properly for me.

---

### Post by rliegh on 2007-11-08
The 2.6 Kernel -on any distribution. I'm giving it another shot (because I want to play with KVM), but up until now I've tried it on several different computers, from several different distributions, and each time I've tried to run it I got sick of it's instability and crashes. 

It's disconcerting when you boot up your OS and you see detailed crash messages because the kernel freaks out trying to load some module or another. 

I've had nothing but bad luck, instability and crashes from 2.6; touch wood that with 2.6.22 and beyond, combined with a store-bought computer I'll *finally* have a working version.

Vista really *isn't* that bad, the main problem with it, in all honesty, is that it is slow as arctic molasses. It came bundled on this computer and I've played with it -XP is more responsive (by an order of magnitude!) but Vista is not anywhere near as bad as you guys make it out to be. 

No idea about Windows ME; glad I managed to dodge that bullet. :lolflag:

### Post by rliegh on 2007-11-08
> **jrharvey said:**
> Vista isnt really that bad. I will say that XP sp2 is better though. Vista is slow but if you turn of all the fancy stuff if does speed up quite a bit. I agree, Even with Aero turned off and using the basic theme, it's not anywhere near as fast as XP sp2. 

The main advantages (as I see it) that Vista has over XP sp2 is native support for newer AMD cpus -but it's no big thing to download the drivers from amd.com and the performance gain makes it worth the trip> 

I still think windows 2000 was probably the best Windows OS. XP was prob #2 but I HATED all the freakin popup bullcrap about no virus protection and firewall crap (even when I didnt have internet enabled). 
FYI you can change that; just click on the balloon and then look over to the left at the window that pops up; you'll see something along the lines of "change the way that windows alerts me" and click that, then uncheck the relevant check boxes. If you're feeling brave you can also disable the service entirely (winkey+R, services.msc and then look for the security service, sorry I don't remember the exact name -google probably has it though if you need it).

:guitar:

### Post by mivo on 2007-11-08
> **jrharvey said:**

I cannot confirm that on my machine. I have turned on quite a few of the effects, use the cube all the time, have AWN running, etc, and the system is very snappy. With a video playing at 1680x1050, Evolution, Swiftweasel, Deluge, some terminals, PuTTY, Pidgin and some other stuff running, the system still uses less than 500-600 MB of RAM. I also did not have a single crash even though I do not reboot daily, and the machine is running 24/7. So, in short, I have a good, rock-stable version of Ubuntu with plenty of functional (or at least pretty!) eye candy. 

I'm not using Vista, so I cannot say how it would run on the same box under similar circumstances. But I can say that Ubuntu runs extremely well. I really notice no difference between "all eye candy" and "everything disabled" in terms of responsiveness and performance. (The box does have a fair amount of memory and a decent video card.)

### Post by LinuxGuy1234 on 2007-11-08
First up, Windows 98/98 SE/ME. It sucked. Badly. Next up, Windows XP and Vista. It sucked so badly. I got loads of blue screens of deaths and viruses, adware and spyware. Would you like someone that used used Bill's OS and get mad at Bill Gates? Ubuntu and other stuff under VirtualBox is what I use now. So much better.

Oh yeah, fix (use a startup disk, maybe even FreeDOS! (LOL) in order to get rid of M$ stuff):
```

cd c:
format c:
fdisk /mbr

```

Or even better:
```

cd c:/windows/system32
del explorer.exe
del lsass.exe
del winlogon.exe
del wpa.dbl
```

:shock: I hadn't even heard of it!! I went straight to Wikipedia to see what that was about.

> Windows CE (also known officially as Windows Embedded CE since version 6.0) (and sometimes abbreviated WinCE) is a variation of Microsoft's Windows operating system for minimalistic computers and embedded systems. Windows CE is a distinctly different kernel, rather than a trimmed-down version of desktop Windows. It is not to be confused with Windows XP Embedded which is NT-based. It is supported on Intel x86 and compatibles, MIPS, ARM, and Hitachi SuperH processors.

I really NEED to see it in action!!

### Post by WhimpyPeon on 2007-11-09
Windows 3.0 and 3.1 were terrible.  We used AS400 5250 emulator cards to connect to the AS400 via twinax cables.  Getting these cards to work was pure voodoo.  Some would work out of the box and others would remain buggy until the day we threw them away.

Back then even getting a modem to work properly was a drag.

As much as I like to bash M$, 95, 98, NT (to some extent), 2000, XP and Vista are all a joy over the 3.X series.

I love Linux in it's current state, but if you go back to the very first time I tried Red Hat in the mid 90's, it was a disaster then as well.  Device drivers were limited the workings of the system were a mystery (for a newbie) and I never did get it to work right.  I scrapped Linux until the early years of Gentoo when I tried again and things were good.  People always consider Gentoo as a distro for experts, but I would argue it is the best way there is to learn Linux (other than perhaps LFS).

I still run two servers with Gentoo, however I generally stick with Debian for servers and Ubuntu/XUbuntu for desktops.

### Post by SuperMike on 2007-11-09
OS/2 Warp. I had to support that beast in a weak moment in my past where I did tech support at IBM ages and ages ago. They hired me as a Novell CNE, but then the jerks stuck me on OS/2 Warp integration with Netware. I hated the Warp network.ini and protocol.ini files and IBM management was very cruel to me. In order to straighten out one's OS/2 Warp's messed up network stuff for complex arrangements, you had to forward the customer's network.ini and protocol.ini to an asian girl in some dark corner. She was a prima donna and took her own sweet time fixing the files. She managed to do it, but it was crazy. I think she had a connection with the actual OS/2 Warp programmers and could do undocumented stuff with their help.

IBM also forced me to brag about MCI (MCA?) architecture versus PCI and I wasn't very good at lying. I also hated how slow it was to thunk open Win32 apps on Warp. When I saw that NationsBank (now Bank of America) purchased a huge Warp contract right before Win95 was released, I had to bust up laughing. They eventually switched to XP when it was released.

### Post by ardchoille42 on 2007-11-09
My worst OS?
Microsoft Windows

Why?
**1)** Virus explanation: [http://www.theregister.co.uk/2003/10/06/linux_vs_windows_viruses/](http://www.theregister.co.uk/2003/10/06/linux_vs_windows_viruses/)

**2)** Microsoft Windows is closed source software.
If someone who wrote the code were paid to collect every keystroke you make on your computer (social security number, mother's maiden name, user names, passwords, credit card information, etc.), you wouldn't know it was happening and you'd be helpless to stop it.

**3) **
**Cost: **
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - More than $100.00 USD. Usually pre-installed on computers leaving the user with no choice. 
[COLOR="Red"]Linux[/COLOR] - $0.00 USD. Freely available via the internet. User has many choices as to kernel, program selection, package manager, user interface configurations and much more. 

**Software: **
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - Most additional software must be purchased and is not maintained by Microsoft. 
[COLOR="Red"]Linux[/COLOR] - Most software is free to download, install and redistribute. 

**Viruses: **
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - 100+ 
[COLOR="Red"]Linux[/COLOR] - None active. 

**Updates: **
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - Updates are routinely delayed until "Patch Tuesday" with some updates delayed for years. 
[COLOR="Red"]Linux[/COLOR] - Most updates distributed within hours. 

**Installation: **
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - Requires activation. Can only be installed on one computer at a time unless the user purchases more licenses. Too many changes in hardware can require re-activation. 
[COLOR="Red"]Linux[/COLOR] - No activation required. Can be installed on millions of computers simultaneously without purchasing or violating any licenses. 

**Distribution: **
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - Re-distribution by the user is forbidden. 
[COLOR="Red"]Linux[/COLOR] - Re-distribution by the user is encouraged.

**4)** Verdict:
[COLOR="Red"]Windows[/COLOR] - sucks
[COLOR="Red"]Linux [/COLOR]- rocks!

---

### Post by SteveHillier on 2007-11-12
Just browsed this thread for a few minutes and I think, hey some of you guys haven't lived yet!!!!
Ok, I skipped out Win ME.  It never seemed to do anything that 98 did so what was the point.  But most of you start with Win 95 and go forward from that.
Those of you who complained about Win 3.1 miss a point or two. 3.1 was a definite improvement on Win 3.0, but I cannot see anyone complaining about Win 2.0.  Now there was a waste of time.  It came out about the same time as IBM OS2 and did not confer any advantages to DOS 6 that was around at the same time.
I have also had the good fortune to never have used any Apple created computer since the Apple IIe.
I did use CP/M on a PC in the early days.  It was a bit of a cross between the DEC OSes and was a hybrid built for a PC.  It had a short life so that should be a contender.
So if I can classify and grade
On DEC Minis probably RT11 - Only able to create new files half the size of the largest contiguous free space - a bit of a b....r when disks were only 5Mb.
On PC probably anything between and including DOS 3 and DOS 5.
On GUIs probably Win 2.0
On Linux probably SUSE 7.0
On computers - any OS you like!!!

### Post by ofb on 2007-11-12
Gentle Reminder Time - this thread is for the OS you *personally* disliked the most, for whatever reason, which you're most welcome to fill us in on. It's *not* about deciding the actual technical merit of an OS. It's an opportunity for people to tell us their own stories.

For example I say Win3.1 was a low point, though I'll happily agree it wasn't all that bad -- I used it till fairly recently on an 8mb laptop. But it also represents a personal low point because I first met it when going from Amiga to PC. What a ghastly let-down that was.

Please do chat about remarks, but also please let's avoid stooping to argument like that nonsense a few pages back over ME. That was OT and just discourages people.

----

And yes! yes indeed, Win3.1 is not technically an OS. Blame me for starting the loose talk, but I just couldn't think of any other succinct term to mean, and be understood to mean, *'the system we operate'* for the purpose of this thread.

So what *should* we call it? What would be a good term that would mean the larger user interface that would include Ubuntu, Win3, and whatever-it-was we used on proto-PCs like the VIC20? I certainly don't like abusing the term 'OS', but I'm still at a loss for thinking of better. It's a poser.

---

> **toupeiro said:**

Not at all. You're hardly the first to mention it in this thread, so I figured it was time to step up to take blame & make clarification. 

As for mentioning it at the beginning, I nearly did & thankfully thought better of it. Long introductions discourage a lot of people, which would have been at odds with the intent. So I went with stating "OS", then followed with the deliberate misapplication to Win3.1 to set the tone as "rather sloppy".

I think it worked. The point was to make people remember their personal villain directly, rather than sidetrack over considerations of best terminology. There's less harm spurring digression now that the bulk has avoided that.

### Post by toupeiro on 2007-11-12
> **ofb said:**
> 
Where does DOS 4 fit in, btw? I can't remember it directly. Roughly when & what did it run on?

DOS 4 was basically a leaky version of DOS, and back when you only had 640K of conventional ram to work with, this was not a good thing.  It was the first time DOSShell could be seen, but the bugs in the OS made any practical use of a feature in this release impossible until 4.01.  It ran on your standard IBM-PC-AT system, but DOS 3.3 was much more stable.  DOS had its first real quantum leap with 5.0, and again with 6.22.

---

### Post by mivo on 2007-11-16
> **xArv3nx said:**
> Gutsy Gibbon. Just closing a ******* tab made Firefox freeze. (Twice) Vista > Ubuntu.

People already pointed out that this is not an OS issue. I never had Firefox crash on me in Linux (but many times in Windows), though it did hang for a few seconds sometimes. I fixed this by using the Linux-optimized version, Swiftweasel, and currently toy around with Epiphany, which, while not being as fully featured, integrates better in Gnome and is overall lighter (I also feel it renders fonts better, though it too uses the Gecko engine). It does need the extensions, though, just for the ad blocker.

You see, Linux gives you many choices. All you have to do is make use of that freedom. If you would rather not (many people are suspicious of real freedom and feel lost or overwhelmed), you can always use what you are familiar with.

### Post by xArv3nx on 2007-11-16
> **mivo said:**
> People already pointed out that this is not an OS issue. I never had Firefox crash on me in Linux (but many times in Windows), though it did hang for a few seconds sometimes. I fixed this by using the Linux-optimized version, Swiftweasel, and currently toy around with Epiphany, which, while not being as fully featured, integrates better in Gnome and is overall lighter (I also feel it renders fonts better, though it too uses the Gecko engine). It does need the extensions, though, just for the ad blocker.

You see, Linux gives you many choices. All you have to do is make use of that freedom. If you would rather not (many people are suspicious of real freedom and feel lost or overwhelmed), you can always use what you are familiar with.
It is an OS thing, because I use Vista and it works fine on it.

You don't have freedom, check out GPL v3. ;)

### Post by Frak on 2007-11-16
> **xArv3nx said:**
> Agreed.

Probably because of the controlled hardware, though.
Controlled hardware helps, but OS X is the epitome of user friendly. It does have some rough spots, such as how the dock handles simultaneous instances of a program. But, I'd rather place an iMac in front of somebody before Ubuntu. Not saying Ubuntu is bad, it's just not there *yet*.

If Windows was controlled hardware, I would say they are all bad OS's, because I don't think it would run any better IMO. 2000 and XP were their best OS's, again IMHO, and Vista is getting there. (Yes, I said getting there; remember, a company will do whatever it takes to make a buck, even if it means making all hardware work on a higher profile)
ME was just a mistake on MS part, and they are going to make sure it doesn't happen again.

Ubuntu, and GNU/Linux in general, is a great OS that will pave the way of the future. Is it user friendly, yes. Is it capable to be used by the average person, yes. Is is ready to be put on the market, not really. I'm not saying no, I'm just saying that without much commercial backing, it's hard for anything to make it in the business "Get it, Get it done, Get it done fast" world. It would be ready to be put on the desktop if it wasn't for everyone being slightly weary to change from what has worked over much time. This follows the old principle, that "You don't fix something if it isn't broken."

### Post by ofb on 2007-11-23
> **ugm6hr said:**
> AmigaOS ... the early versions were so compact they happily loaded from floppy.

Remember the feeling of *luxury* when you got a second floppy drive?

That and a 2MB RAM expansion made the A1000 feel like a dream machine for a while.

(For the age-deficient: Said expansion was a rather expensive metal box attached to the sideport, probably containing two ISA cards loaded with small chips. This was before harddrives, and a stock A1000 contained 256-512K RAM -- 2MB was a huge increase in capability.)

I don't know which one to blame, but if an SD card slot or the thumbpad work only occasionally someone has to take the blame. Good thing the reset button usually works.

The biggest thing that XP brought to the table for me was the lack of crashes.  I think XP was/is okay, although it's definitely showing it's age now.  I've never used Vista.

**User:** Delete Java 1.5.0 then!
**openSUSE:** Are you mad? You have Java-based apps on this freshly installed machine
**User:** ... Is there any way you can replace Java 1.5.0 with the new version?
**openSUSE:** Here's a pitchfork, stick it in your eyes and wait for my big brother, version 11

### Post by *daniel on 2007-12-28
The first OS I ever used was (I think) an IBM DOS version of some sort. Then MS-DOS. Then Windows 3.0. Then Windows 3.1 (which wasn't bad, though I had nothing to compare it to, of course).

Since then I've tried (this is what I do for fun sometimes) Solaris, OpenBSD, FreeBSD, Macintosh OSX (because my wife has one for school), Macintosh OS 9, Suse Linux 8, Fedora Core 4, Damn Small Linux, Xubuntu, Kubuntu, Ubuntu... and Windows 95, 95, NT, ME, 2000, XP, 2003, Vista, and I look forward to trying 2008.

What I really want is to go back and try some of the pre-PC Unixes, just to see what they were like. 

In the meantime I think my least favourite operating system experience was Windows ME. It wasn't just that it lacked functionality, or that it was confusing: it was a little, but it looked pretty much like 95/98. It was that it crashed all the time over the smallest thing. I couldn't believe that with the rich history the world has in operating system design such a steaming pile of manure was actually released. As a product. To consumers. 

Second worst was Suse 8. I don't know what it was, but nothing seemed to work. It was hell. 

Third worst has been (thus far), Vista. I think I know what they were trying to do with Vista, but it seems like instead of doing that, they did something else. For instance, see the new control panel; it's like they looked at Gnome and thought, "Hey, configuration applet proliferation looks pretty cool!" Because they've definitely beaten Gnome at its own game. On the other hand, it looks pretty (finally!).

Fourth worst was Fedora Core 4. Tied with Windows 95.

---

### Post by BradwJensen on 2007-12-28

As much as i'd love to use the OS's over Windows and Mac, it's just not as reliable.  So many tweaks are needed for it to run DECENT..  I'm tired of spending forever setting things up to work sometimes decently and not better than that.  To install a program is a huge pain in the ars when its not released in a deb, or in the synaptic.  **Compiling things are what should be for developers and not for users.** Sorry, but it's true..

Windows 95 & 98se even worked more properly than all 3 of these, in many places..

I will say that windows ME sucked about just as bad in reliability.

It's a shame i have to compare Windows ME to these potential Open Source OS's..

### Post by Frak on 2007-12-28
> **BradwJensen said:**
> I REALLY hate to have to say this, but so far it's been  Ubuntu, OpenSUSE, and LinuxMINT...

As much as i'd love to use the OS's over Windows and Mac, it's just not as reliable.  So many tweaks are needed for it to run DECENT..  I'm tired of spending forever setting things up to work sometimes decently and not better than that.  To install a program is a huge pain in the ars when its not released in a deb, or in the synaptic.  **Compiling things are what should be for developers and not for users.** Sorry, but it's true..

Windows 95 & 98se even worked more properly than all 3 of these, in many places..

I will say that windows ME sucked about just as bad in reliability.

It's a shame i have to compare Windows ME to these potential Open Source OS's..

Don't bash me, I'm just giving my own HONEST opinion from using all OS's for at least a FEW months each, more so for Windows and Ubuntu.
Same with Windows - **Compiling things are what should be for developers and not for users.**
They provide you .exe, .msi, and .bin
If you want to, you can use a multitude of compilers. Such as Microsoft Developers Studio.

Same with Macintosh - **Compiling things are what should be for developers and not for users.**
They provide you .dmg, .pkg, and .bin
If you want to, you can use XCode to compile for Mac, along with custom installers.

Same with Linux - **Compiling things are what should be for developers and not for users.**
They provide you with .deb, .rpm, .run, .autopackage, .klik, .bin, and pre-binaries
If you want to, you can use GCC to compile.

I've NEVER had to compile anything for Linux for it to run correctly. I've NEVER had to compile anything for Windows for it to run correctly. I've NEVER had to compile anything for OS X for it to run correctly.

---

Just a quick example of what i think of..  Installing vmware server on Windows is as simple as download .exe and install then everything works perfectly, nothing holding you back from going forward... On Linux you have to download the files, compile them, install them, and then if it hasn't asked you to be root to compile it and install it you sometimes still can't write a virtual OS to your computer because you happen to don't have write permissions on that place where you want the OS written..  what a pain..  it should be as simple for a end user to just download the installer, install it, and it works with no more problems with the OS's ability to work with the App..

It's like that for tons of things.  It took me forever to be able to get a deb of Avant Windows Navigator and get all the dependencies installed for it to work.

Internet browsers don't work anywhere near as correct in Linux as on Windows..  On OpenSUSE  png's with transparencies show dark spots on the site you're lookin at, and in all Linux OS's, if you go to a site with a lot on it, it scrolls like crap - almost impossible to scroll down, even after its all done loading...  Yahoo.com doesn't even display correctly on linux in any web browser..

Firefox 3 is supposed to show websites "perfectly" says the Acid2 test, and it does on windows xp.. but in linux, nope, it looks messed up.

Those are just a few things to name off why its not that reliable.. **mainly its the programs built on top of the OS, not really the OS itself... But without those apps on the top, what use is the OS anyways?  **They need to work together seamlessly for it to be a good OS.

---

### Post by bmbernie on 2007-12-29
Hopefully this doesn't turn into a flame war but there are some things that should be cleared up.

I have seen some posts saying that windows just works and Linux doesn't.  When you do an install of any version of windows it isnt working out of the box.  There are many things that we all do when installing an operating system after a fresh install.  Mainly this is downloading updates and installing them.

For example after I installed Windows XP I had to install the special drivers for my motherboards nvidia chipset in order to get their IDE driver installed, and to install the network bridge drivers to get Ethernet working.  Then I had to install the special sound drivers.

After that I needed to install a fix for my dual core amd processor.  

Then my video card drivers.

So.. its not so plug and play.  

In linux the experience was quite similar after you install your distro you may have to update the system and get all the critical fixes, and then install any specialized drivers that you may need.

---

### Post by NineseveN on 2007-12-29
If we want to start recently, then the DRM wrapper and spyware that they call "Vista" is certainly the worst current OS for a number of reasons (performance, support, DRM, anti-privacy, anti-user etc...).

If we go back to the previous versions of Windows, I can say that Microsoft almost hit a home run with Windows XP, if it wasn't for the misguided efforts to combat piracy, which ended up affecting the end user to a degree too great to be called "reasonable". If you were lucky enough to get a "corporate edition" copy of XP and use a valid (i.e. purchased) CD key, then all you had to do was subscribe to Steve Gibson's newsletter and use a variety of programs to close security risks shipped by default with XP (UPNP, DCOM/RPC, Windows Messenger, the URL exploit fixed by XPdite), remain vigilant with your anti-virus and malware/spyware programs and hope that security updates would come more frequently (which in some cases they did, and in some, they didn't). Sure, XP mostly worked out of the box with common software (not games though, which is a fault of the hardware and game developers as much as it is with MS), but "working" and "working well" are two different things. Not only did the end-user have to close a number of security loopholes in XP, but they also had to shutdown and disable 20 or more services running by default that they did not need, did not want, would cause conflicts or open security holes. So sure I had to install some extras to get flash running in Firefox/Swiftweasel and play DVD movies, and sure I had to copy and paste a few lines in a configuration file to get my 5-button mouse to work, but I only needed to shut down a handful of services from the System>Administration>Services and System>Preferences>Sessions menu, which overall is a bit less work than I ever needed to do in Windows XP on a clean install (especially less than when XP first came out and the Service Packs to fix *some* of those issues only to introduce others of course).

Or, of course, we could go back to the absolute disaster that was Windows ME. Nothing good can really be said about that piece of expensive garbage except that it ranks high on most all-time lists for worst Operating Systems, software or PC/technology blunders.

Windows 95 was cutting edge and a good start, though terribly buggy (though, that's like any software for the most part). Windows 98 was okay once it matured, so long as one was vigilant and savvy, but it was a garbled mess at launch. Of course, the bar was much lower for an OS at that time as computers hadn't really fully penetrated the entire home market yet, so the issues were ones that mostly geeks could figure out. Towards the end of Win98's lifespan, more and more regular folks were getting computers, which opened us a whole new series of issues brought on by problems that non-tech savvy people could not solve easily on their own.

In the Windows world, I actually found myself longing for the days of Win95 or Windows 92 Second Edition (a really good release for MS standards, how the OS should have shipped in the first place), when the worst problems with the OS for a non-nerd came from installing AOL and other third-party applications that ruined your system or threw your computer into the Blue-Screen-Of-Death chasm at will.

The fact that they're phasing out XP for reasons other than actual software or hardware advances that are beneficial to the actual user is a shame.


The problem with *nix has always been how far behind it has been from Windows in the realm of common desktop usability. There has never been any question that I know of regarding which OS is more powerful in the hands of an expert user, but Joe Blow wasn't going to be able to handle *nix without swallowing a make-shift tech degree just to get it to do what he wanted it to. Thankfully, that is no longer the case. Windows 95 was miles and miles ahead of *nix for common desktop usage, now the difference between Windows and *nix is based merely upon the distro one uses. Ubuntu or Fedora, for example, are not perfect by any means, but they are on par with the launch version of Windows XP, and in many areas, they exceed it (security and basic stability). That no flavor of *nix is directly comparable to Windows Vista is more of a badge of honor than an insult.


As a side note about games, that was one of the things holding me back for so long from going completely to *nix, but with the crap that passes for gaming these days, I bought a console and haven't looked back. For what it costs to upgrade a Windows Vista machine (or a former XP powerhouse converted to Vista) to run cutting edge or even just new games, one could buy a Nintendo Wii or the lower tier systems from Sony or even Microsoft. Though, a number of the most popular games will currently run on *nix with some effort so long as they're not directly linked in with Microsoft's DirectX 10 at the moment.
__________________

---

### Post by pbpersson on 2008-03-15
I used CPM, DOS, Win3.1, OS/2, Win95, Win98, WinME, Windows NT 3.1 through 4.0, Windows 2000, Windows XP, and of course SEVERAL versions of Linux

The funny thing is how much my perceptions change over the years

When I first started with OS/2 I thought it was the greatest thing since sliced bread.  Then once I got used to Windows NT....when I went back to OS/2 it seemed very clunky and hard to use.

Then for years Windows NT was my OS of choice - so high tech and easy to use.  Then I got used to Windows 2000/XP and plug and play.

One day because I didn't have enough licenses here for all the machines (yes, I do keep track of that - I am strange) I installed NT 4.0 on one machine.  I had to MANUALLY get all the drivers and MANUALLY install them all.  I was HORRIFIED!!!  I used to think that OS was high tech????  :lolflag:

I would say my worst ever experience with any OS was the first time I ever tried Linux.  I think it was Red Hat 6.2.  Nothing worked, everything crashed, I could not figure out how to install anything and once I did install something it said "sucessfully installed" but I had no icon to use it and had no idea where the application had been installed.

There was lots of help on the web......but I needed help to understand the help.  Most of the help said "XYZ your JKL then UKMSRT and recompile your kernel - it was like a different language.  There was SO MUCH that was broken and did not work that I quickly rushed back to the safety and sanity of Windows.

### Post by ygolohcysp on 2008-03-18
I would say Win ME almost without a doubt, but at least in the short segments that it did run, you could actually do things with it.  Vista has to be the worst.  I'd rate 98 as higher than XP pro though.  There were several .vxd files 98 needed, which were provided on the install CD but for some reason never got installed in any version of 98.  If you took those out of the cabs and put them in the system and system32 directories, 98 would run stable for months at a time with no reboot.  And it was possible to get 98 to recognize and use 2 gig of RAM.  With XP though, the more updates you do, the more likely it gets broken, and if not, the useability goes down. I honestly like Ubuntu the most, though I haven't tried any other recent distro's of Linux.  Unfortunately, for computer use, I also have to consider programs alongside of the OS.  It's dual boot for me with this and XP.

### Post by Bungo Pony on 2008-03-18

OPEN 15,8,15: PRINT#15,"N:FLOPPY NAME, ID#22": CLOSE 15

I think I got it right :)

I loved using the C-64, including BASIC. The actual (D)OS was stored in the floppy drive. You could also run GeOS if you hated using BASIC.

### Post by uswingu on 2008-03-22
MVS was a big, stinkin' hunk o' cheese - as inflexible as an OS could hope to be.  At least it was stable.

Early versions of MacOS and Windows were pretty bad, but arguably no worse (in a relative sense, at least) than early versions of Mandrake Linux with KDE.

I can't bring myself to bag on DOS or CP/M, but did think the TI OSes of that era were crap, remarkably unstable and buggy for a commercial product.  One of my first tech jobs was as a sort of sales engineer, demonstrating the TI-99 series for prospective small business customers...I still shudder when I think about it.

Vista?  Come on, it's not that bad.  Sure, it is a resource hog - but really, people need to stop clinging to the 80386sx and get a modern box anyway.  The new filesystem is worlds better than NTFS, the GUI is far more intuitive than OS X, and the whole thing is, well, about what you'd expect from Microsoft.  It's not an engineering triumph but it'll get the job done.

---

### Post by isecore on 2008-03-22
> **uswingu said:**
> Vista?  Come on, it's not that bad.  Sure, it is a resource hog - but really, people need to stop clinging to the 80386sx and get a modern box anyway.  The new filesystem is worlds better than NTFS, the GUI is far more intuitive than OS X, and the whole thing is, well, about what you'd expect from Microsoft.  It's not an engineering triumph but it'll get the job done.

What new filesystem? As far as I know Vista still runs on bad old NTFS, a filesystem that can't hold a candle to anything Linux runs on. WinFS would've been neat, but it was axed years ago.

As for "clinging to the 386sx" I'm running a Phenom quad-core with 4 gigs of RAM, and Vista won't even install. It bluescreens right before loading the graphics interface and sprouts some crap about my bios not being 100% acpi compliant. I wanted to give it a go mainly since it has DirectX10 (I only use Windows for games) but even when I voluntarily wanted to install it there was no go.

If there was anything one can say about Vista is that it didn't live up to the hype. Sure, it'll "get the job done" but at what expense? It's a bloated suckfest that should've been aborted years ago. It's confusing to new as well as old users of Windows, and it requires silly amounts of computing power to perform the same things that XP already does. A new operating system should bring something new of additional value to the user, and Vista doesn't do that. It brings a flashy interface and nothing else. It's a glorified and very expensive service-pack to XP, and even XP took several years befor it got usable.

### Post by DayOldPorridge on 2008-03-22
Vector Linux, for sure.

When I tried logging in to the Live CD it required me to log in first, and although the password was pretty obvious ("vectorlinux"), it took me at least two tries to get in.  I admit, I should've read the documentation first, but seriously, if they want to make it more user friendly maybe they could just get rid of the login screen on the Live CD.

After that, I was going to try installing it, but I found that there was no way to do this from the Live CD. Oh, and my wireless card wasn't detected automatically, so it was pretty useless.  Luckily, though, I had downloaded the core .iso beforehand, so I ended up making Grub boot the .iso straight from my Ubuntu partition.  The install was pretty fast, I admit.  After I had gotten it up and running, I then had to install ndiswrapper to get the wireless to work.  

Now, what I usually did with OS's that didn't come with this by default was just drag and drop the ndiswrapper installer and Atheros drivers straight from the Ubuntu desktop to the other OS's desktop via Nautilus.  I couldn't do this with Vector, though, as I didn't even have "permission" to access the Desktop on there.  So then I booted back into Vector to try and get ndiswrapper and the drivers off the Ubuntu desktop and onto Vector's.

But guess what?  There's absolutely no way to access your other partitions via the WM on Vector.  I guess if I'd known a bit more of Linux when I tried it out, I probably would've figured out a way to access the other partitions some other way.  But seriously, not giving the users graphical access to their other partitions through the WM is such a stupid move, especially when the OS is just starting out.  It was definitely not one of the fastest OS's I'd ever tried, either, as I've heard it was supposed to be.

Second would probably be Sidux; much of the same problems regarding wireless, and just a bit overrated as far as speed goes.  Oh, and might I mention I ran into quite a few dependency problems once I did get wireless up and running, as they didn't necessarily have the largest repositories I'd seen.

### Post by TheKorn2 on 2008-03-25
Ubuntu.  

Alsa is a ***running disaster***; every time I reboot my machine I have no idea if the sound is going to die, why, or for how long.  It comes back for a while, then it goes away...    Oh, you switched from a VGA to a HDMI cable going to *the same device*?  Well NO SOUND FOR YOU!

I guess the saving grace is that I didn't pay for Ubuntu.  But I certainly know where the "linux is not ready for the desktop" idea comes from -- they're right!  Every time it happens I burn a day or two searching through google and the alsa bugs, chasing down dark alley after dark alley.

I've had enough of it.  Windows XP (despite being bloatware) ***actually works reliably***.  By that, I mean once you get a system working, by and large it stays working.  Ubuntu (and especially alsa) can't say that.  Things can and DO break of their own accord for no logical reason whatsoever, and even if you pound google until it's blue in the face *the only solution is to format and start over*.


Sorry, I know it's an ubuntu forum, but let's call a spade a spade here.  When I was in my teens, this would have been fun.  In my twenties, I'd probably still enjoy it.  I'm in my thirties now, and spending *days* trying to chase down *simple* bugs just is ridiculous.  It's not 1992 anymore.


If MythTV ran on windows I'd be there in half a heartbeat.

### Post by Blue_Lander on 2008-03-26
Fedora Core. 

WAIT! Let me explain

I had JUST begun to understand what Linux was, and was excited to try it out. I think i was about 12-13 at the time (yeah I know, I'm a kid). I jumped the gun so much I barely realized there were different distros or even that they were free. So, I went to a book store and bought a Linux book with an OS disc inside. It happened to be the *week* that Fedora Core had come out. Little did I know, I was about to recieve a painful mental beating from my good friend Tux. There I was, barely a teenager, fighting to figure out even the most basic things in an OS that hadn't even recieved it's first patch yet.

Luckily, it didn't scare me off from Linux as a whole (I've even revisited Fedora recently, to amend our relationship :P) and it even got me interested in programming. So in a way my worst OS was also my most influental OS.

### Post by xxxamazexxx on 2008-03-30
You may find my post off-putting, insulting, rubbish, but
I dislike Ubuntu (and Linux in general) very much.
At first I fell in love with it. But then
Sound card didn't work
Graphic card didn't work
Bluetooth didn't work
WLAN struggled to work
Irrecoverable crashes
I feel that Linux is not on the right track. It must have unique features (like it does now) but also has to maintain connection with the outside world
For example, Linux has to be installed on an ext partition. That involves a painstakingly considerable effort of formatting drives which can easily lead to conflicts. I'm have deleted all the ext drives but can't make new partition on the free space freed by them.
Microsoft is famous for Windows' crashes and errors and bugs .. but they always manage to recover. My comp has crashed on Windows countless times but so far I haven't lost a byte. Let's look at Linux. Yesterday I was trying to install driver for my sound card (which I had to dig up Google to find where it is and how to install it). After installing my comp crashed. I can still run other programs, but the installation windows hung. Then I restarted and got a s-t-u-p-i-d-e-s-t error notice I've ever seen: Your last session lasted for less than 10 minutes blah blah bla Try this try that blah blah blah but nothing is recovered. 
For a simple error the whole system crashes. Is that what is expected in Linux ?
Last week I got some source-code program to install in Linux. SH, BIN, TAR ... each of them requires a specific way to install. I couldn't afford that.
Now I can't go with Ubuntu (and Linux) any further than the logon screen.
Now what i'm trying to do is to use XP and find a way to recover the lost space of my hard drive.

---

Linux is a hard-hat area. Don't be afraid to get nitty-gritty with the system, and don't mind to get technical with it.

You may want to try Freespire. It may be actually having better hardware support.

### Post by Shakey_Jake33 on 2008-04-02
Probably Windows ME, though I have little experience with many Linux distros outside Ubuntu, so it's not really a fair comment.  I think the fact that so many people (myself included) used Windows 2000 as a desktop OS rather than ME is quite telling though.

As an OS (i.e putting the FOSS stuff aside for a second), I have little hate for Vista.  It's certainly the most stable Windows OS I've used, especially when you look at the state of XP pre-SP1.  Where Vista fails in my mind is simply the requirements... when it feel like your computer is devoting more resources to the OS than the program you're running on top of it, you know there's something wrong.  The OS should be very much in the background to the program you're running (imo).

### Post by tjniels on 2008-04-11
Now, I know there are worse versions of Windows, but the only one I used for more than a week or two was XP.  I rented a 2.6 GHz P4 Dell with 1 GB of RAM from my university for a semester, they had just done a clean install and wiped the HD, but that thing was as slow as an overloaded garbage truck!  Being a long-time Mac user, I had a crash course in anti-virus software as well, lol.  When I bought my own 500MHz G3 iMac, it felt twice as fast!

My second worst OS would have to be DOS on an Apple ][e, I was already used to the Macintosh Finder and then I had to go back to a green screen and typing every darn thing...  Kinda funny, because these days that's one of my favorite things about UNIX!

A few people bashed on the old Mac OS, but the 7.x and earlier systems (like Finder 1.0 that I booted from floppy on my 128k original Mac) have always been a favorite of mine.  Simplistic, but basically the basis of every GUI out there today, like it or not.  OK, OK, there was Xerox, but they don't make an OS anymore.  When comparing Mac OS to Windows of the same year, Windows always felt primitive for me.  I gave them their very last chance last year with that Pentium 4 Dell.  What's crazy is that Vista is even slower!

I've had some bad experiences with *ubuntu on PPC architecture, but that's simply because I'm installing unofficial distributions and fixing several things myself.  I like *ubuntu once I get it running properly, I'm sure that would be easier on a more generic PC.  But hey, Debian seems to work great on several of my PPC computers, so the linux world has not completely abandoned me!  :)

Don't get me wrong, XP is respectable, but compared to the alternatives?  Just say "No" to the BSOD!  Yes, XP bluescreens and so does Vista.

### Post by bohica8418 on 2008-04-13
I would have to say windows mobile is among the worst I've used. But I think it may be the HTC 8125 that's so slow on which I'm using it. 
To respond to an earlier user who pointed out almost all of disliked systems are windows, I would like to offer a rebuttal. 
 
I  have used windows starting with 95. I have used OSX extensively and helped set up our schools network of Macs. I have run Ubuntu on my laptop for the past year. 

   Windows has a unique market share which inevitably shines a negative light on it. It must work on all mainstream PCs. For that reason it must work across a huge spectrum of hardware. Those hardware manufacturers to a large degree dictate the stability of Windows, they may write horrible drivers with intentional bugs, such as Creative. On the opposite side they may open their drivers up to be open-source, such as ATI.
 --An anecdote along those lines: I was running Vista, I never had major problems with it, it ran smoothly, well, and its directX10 support made it a good gaming OS. I was extremely puzzled by the almost unanimous agreement that it was horribly unstable. Then I stumbled across and article that explained it all. Nvidia's drivers were solely responsible for between 20 and 30 percent of all vista crashes. I was using an ATI vid-card. It made me realize just how dependent Windows is on its third-party drivers--  

   The antithesis of this is OSX. It has always annoyed me to no end when smug Mac fans assert that Macs are just so much more stable. Wellllllll, when your OS is only available on a tiny number of hardware configurations which are manufactured specifically to be compatible I would be disappointed if it was less than perfectly stable. which it is. When setting up my school's network we ran into many many issues. One of the most spectacular was when the whole network spontaneously stopped working. The cause: a security update caused OSX to not recognize our proxy which prohibits students from accessing inappropriate material. Apple's response was to suggest we disable our proxy until the next update, in which it would probably be solved. Well we had no choice, as the only way to remove the update I knew of was to reinstall the OS from scratch, not really an option for more than a hundred computers. Luckily the next update did indeed solve that problem. The moral of that story is that Macs are not perfect, and they are not very sympathetic 

   Finally Windows must overcome the hardest problem. The majority of its users don't know what DVD stands for, let alone GUI. Linux distros like Ubuntu have the distinct advantage that most of their users at least have a good grasp of computing. I defy anyone to create an operating system my father cannot render unusable in six months. He knows nearly nothing about computers, but has happily used windows to browse the internet for several years now. Yet I swear he could mess up the OS on a N64. I usually have to reinstall XP once every six months simply because he has around 200 desktop icons, the resolution changes when he opens the browser, and every time it starts up there are around 20 messages telling various programs can't find this or that. Yet XP has managed to survive his continued assaults without ever actually refusing to run. I shudder to think what he could find to play with under the system tab in ubuntu. 


So in conclusion I want to say:
I bet the vast majority of you consider yourself advanced computer users. So think about what your saying before you turn every discussion of OSs into a Windows flame war. ;)

---

### Post by ofb on 2008-04-17
Reminder time:

> This is a light-hearted thread for the OS you hated. 

It's for people who've used at least three OS on a daily basis over the years. It's not a technical discussion of Pros & Cons, it's just for what you remember the least fondly.

It can be because you hated the box art -- it doesn't matter, but **do** say why because that **is** the point. People have bad experiences for all sorts of odd reasons. That's what this thread is for, so let's hear it.

*It's not a technical discussion of Pros & Cons* because then people will think they have to objectively justify their subjective choice. Then we'll miss the weirder stories, and we'll have a much smaller sampling of what people really didn't like, & why.

'Objective' discussions of the 'best' and 'worst' OS are commonplace, and frankly pretty boring. This thread is not one of those. 

Got it? Great! Carry on.

[I can't believe we have over 700 posts and no one has said UNIX yet. People really used to hate it. Also, I don't think any other OS has escaped vilification -- maybe Apple OSs earlier than 7? Oh, and Palm. Any others?]

---

### Post by Frak on 2008-04-17
> **joe.turion64x2 said:**
> So does Linux, in fact the range of devices Linux runs on is larger (count PPC, SPARC, AMD64 too).
The NT OS can run on many platforms (possibly not as much as Linux, but VERY close)
> A main design goal of NT was hardware and software portability. Versions of NT were available for a variety of processor architectures, namely Intel IA-32, MIPS, Alpha, PowerPC, SPARC, Intel i860, and Intel i960...
And we all know that NT can run natively on AMD64
[Linky]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Windows_NT#Major_features")

### Post by Rhapsody on 2008-04-22
I've never really hated an operating system.

I started with Windows for Workgroups 3.11, which wasn't great, but then it's not like anyone was offering anything better for the desktop back in 1993.

Then there was Windows 95, which was a great improvement, people who knock it are forgetting how much of an improvement it was over Windows 3.x.

Windows 98 was probably the low point in my opinion, the Windows/386 architecture was getting on a bit by then and some of Microsoft's poor decisions with Windows 95 (biggest one: FAT16) were beginning to bite them at that point.

I skipped straight to Windows XP after that, which I must admit is one of Microsoft's finest. Once you kill the Fisher-Price GUI and some unnecessary services, it's a fine and stable operating system.

I also briefly used Windows 2000, with my main impression being that it was like a more sensible version of Windows XP. I can see why people tout it as the best operating system Microsoft ever made.

Then there's Kubuntu, which I love enough to be using now. It's stable, it's got plenty of features, it's not as insane as Vista, and it doesn't cost anything. Perfection.

### Post by MaxIBoy on 2008-05-20
> What's wrong with this market. Every version of windows just keeps on consuming more and more ram. 

It has to do with PC manufacturers wanting to present expensive, overpowered computers as the bare minimum. Microsoft makes an OS that uses up an insane amount of RAM, PC manufacturers have an excuse to sell more expensive PCs. PC manufacturers make more money by charging more, Microsoft sells more units, and everyone is happy except the customer (who spends way more money than is really needed.) 

Just take a moment to consider how much computational horsepower computers have these days. I think that even a cheap Sempron could run Crysis if the OS was actually well designed or wasn't forced to use a Windows Runtime. If, say, DSL had a Crysis port written for it, you'd be able to watch the system requirements go through the floor, basement, AND foundations.

The end result, though, is that computers never run fast enough to handle the flagship OS fully. In other words, the people who use Linux benefit from having it run insanely fast, even on older computers.

---

### Post by wumpus on 2008-05-21
> **dizzie said:**
> Remember the dull piccy showing a cement block?
[deletia]
Dont talk bad about MS-DOS, it (almost) never crashed, unless (i repeat!) unless, you was a fool using stacker, doublespace (or whatever lame name they called it)
[deletia]


I'll talk bad about MS-DOS day and night.  I started with home computers made by Atari (400/800), then jumped straight to SunOS (which showed me what a proper computer was), then I learned about MS-DOS.

To make things worse, my first computer of my own was a 386 running DOS.  DOS was a reasonable excuse for an 8088, and the 80286 didn't deserve a real system, but running MS-DOS on the 386 (and 486 and to some degree pentiums) was a complete waste.  These machines were 32-bit beasts with an honest to God MMU, and they were loaded with a non-reentrant interupt handler instead of an OS.

I don't think there was anything especially bad about 98se, but I know that it plus all the pain I'd gotten from MS over the years pushed me to full time linux.  I will point out to the "linux wasn't ready for the desktop in 2000" skeptics that my prefered method of connecting windows (dual boot, of course) to DSL involved using an old linux (mandrake) machine as a router. The windows DSL drivers were always to flakey.

I'm not sure where SLS linux goes.  All I could do with it and relax that after an eternal download, my computer finally had a real OS on it.  I never expected to do anything with it, but was glad it was there.

Red hat deserves mention on this list, insisting on a proprietary MBR isn't going to ever get the thing loaded on any machine I own.  I am not buying a new hard drive just for red hat.

</rant>

I must say I was kind of "wow'ed" when I saw what games my friends could play on win 95 (Red Alert comes to mind, better graphics). Upgraded to 98SE because of lots of 95's shortcomings (such as restart to change resolution). 
Installed Win2k when apps didn't work for win98 (Vue comes to mind). Using it with bblean was really nice, crashed only once (bad drivers from ATI). At that point I heard of Ubuntu and wanted to try it. Installed Ubuntu 6.04 and noticed I couldn't use my other harddrive (formated to NTFS) and didn't have rights to use my own USB Drives... :S
Removed Ubuntu and was about to put win2k back, but then Dad came home with XP Pro, so it was XP. Was using XP for years until military service kicked in. After three months in service I bought an Acer Aspire 5520 which came preloaded with Vista. First month Vista was super fast, but after installing more apps it got kind of slow, so I dualbooted Linux Mint.
Got fed up with Vista gaming performance and removed Mint and installed XP. Nice for gaming.
The rest you can read above before the edit. Seriously thinking about removing all other OSes than Vista, works like a charm. I might actually get Vista 64 once the prices get abit lower.

### Post by palomar on 2008-07-30
I think it's too easy to say win 9x/ME is crap, comparing it with XP or recent Ubuntu's. For it's time, win9x was quite a good and usable OS. It made computers accessible to the Joe Sixpack people...  Remember popularity of the internet was still growing, so it was not designed to be a 100% safe networking OS... It also crashed sometimes, but most of the time it was caused by 3rd party apps. So if you just watch out *what* software you are installing it won't crash that much.. 

The only thing I hated on win ME was it didn't support real DOS mode for some marketing reasons... (but using a hack fixed that :wink:).

XP is quite a good and stable OS. No complaints about that. It's still my favorite Windows (and perhaps favorite OS...). Vista is OK as well, but a bit bloated. IMO it's XP with some extra apps (defender, indexer, fancy GUI) which I will never install myself. So I switched back to XP after a few months.

Let's talk about Linux now... for desktop usage, the early distro's (pre 2003) were terrible. You had to create partitions yourself, installing software was a hell, dependencies, no consistent GUI (every program has it's own fonts and colors #-o), most of my hardware was not recognized, very slow et cetera..... So everytime after a few days I got very frustrated and quit playing with it. More recent distro's were better at these points, but still you had to do a lot of console work to make things work. I hated that. Finally, since Ubuntu 8.04 almost everything works out of the box and it's the first Linux which I consider ready for the desktop :) I like it very much.

So, my worst OS experiences (for desktop usage) were the early Linux distro's. They were definitely not ready for end users.

### Post by xen-uno on 2008-08-05
Worst for me was OSX 10, probably because I knew how to make any Windows version light, fast, clean, and trouble free. OSX 10 and the awful mouse on the i-mac was something I couldn't get used to despite spending several days trying to get 3 machines (belonging to my brother's kids) talking to each other and to the Airport. The damn settings just wouldn't stick.

I went from DOS 5 to Windows for Workgroups 3.11, briefly to W95, then straight into NT as a beta tester. W2K was my favorite but with XP modded to behave and look the same, I would have to say XP (x64) is my favorite and it is the workhorse here. I'm diggin Ubuntu seriously though and it is my XP replacement when that time comes.

Most admirable OS I've seen to date was the Amiga OS ... it could do so much with so little resources. None of the knock-offs compare to the original Lemmings and Crystal Hammer on the Amiga.

### Post by jcway212 on 2008-08-06
IN ORDER OF EXPERIENCE.

Win95 was my first.....It was OK

Win98.........OK

Win98se.......really had a good experience with that.

WinME.........this ties for my worst.

Win2000.......better then 98se

WinXp.........initially buggy, but came to work fine.

RedHat v?.....many years back my friend tried to get me into this and it scared me half to death! Uninstalled it and stayed away from Linux all together.

Ubuntu Gutsy....My friend told me to try Linux again and I agreed worked with it for awhile and got a bit frustrated, didn't understand the point of going Linux, when my games didn't work :/

Vista....................yay........it sucked, IT WAS THE WORST!!!!!!!

Ubuntu Hardy....Looked at Linux from a development and learning perspective....also looked at open source through a different lens......and I am now addicted.....like cocaine addicted :)

I am now dual booting on my main system with XP and Ubuntu, I will eventually port everything, but there are a few things that I need XP for, for now. My laptop that had Vista on it only has Ubuntu right now. I am thinking about loading Kubuntu on to try that out. Don't know for sure though.
